du">Share</p></div></button></div></div></div></div></div></div></div></div></div><p id="97e4" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mh mi gu mj b mk ml mm mn mo mp mq mr ms mt mu mv mw mx my mz na nb nc nd ne gn bk">By <a class="af nf" href="https://www.linkedin.com/in/natital/" rel="noopener ugc nofollow" target="_blank"><strong class="mj gv">Nati Tal</strong></a>, <a class="af nf" href="https://www.linkedin.com/in/oleg-zaytsev-rd/" rel="noopener ugc nofollow" target="_blank"><strong class="mj gv">Oleg Zaytsev</strong></a><strong class="mj gv"> </strong>(<a class="af nf" href="http://www.guard.io/" rel="noopener ugc nofollow" target="_blank">Guardio Labs</a>)</p><blockquote class="ng nh ni"><p id="16ca" class="mh mi nj mj b mk ml mm mn mo mp mq mr ms mt mu mv mw mx my mz na nb nc nd ne gn bk">Guardio Labs uncovers a sprawling campaign of subdomain hijacking, compromising already over <strong class="mj gv">8,000</strong> domains from esteemed brands and institutions, including <strong class="mj gv">MSN, VMware, McAfee, The Economist, Cornell University, CBS, Marvel, eBay </strong>and others. This malicious activity, dubbed “SubdoMailing”, leverages the trust associated with these domains to circulate spam and malicious phishing emails by the Millions each day, cunningly using their credibility and stolen resources to slip past security measures.</p><p id="01ef" class="mh mi nj mj b mk ml mm mn mo mp mq mr ms mt mu mv mw mx my mz na nb nc nd ne gn bk">In our detailed analysis, we disclose how we detected this extensive subdomain hijacking effort, its mechanisms, its unprecedented scale and the main threat actor behind it. Furthermore, we developedthe <a class="af nf" href="https://guard.io/subdomailing" rel="noopener ugc nofollow" target="_blank">“SubdoMailing” checker </a>— a website designed to empower domain owners to reclaim control over their compromised assets and shield themselves against such pervasive threats. This report not only sheds light on the magnitude of the issue but also serves as a call to action for enhancing domain security against future exploits.</p></blockquote><h1 id="4445" class="nk nl gu bf nm nn no np nq nr ns nt nu nv nw nx ny nz oa ob oc od oe of og oh bk">Thousands of Hijacked Domains — and Counting!</h1><p id="3057" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mh mi gu mj b mk oi mm mn mo oj mq mr ms ok mu mv mw ol my mz na om nc nd ne gn bk">Over recent months, <a class="af nf" href="https://www.guard.io" rel="noopener ugc nofollow" target="_blank">Guardio</a>’s email protection systems have identified unusual patterns in email metadata, particularly concerning SMTP servers and their authentication as legitimate senders. This sparked an investigative journey for our research team, taking us through the inner workings of the SMTP protocol, Domain hunting, developing scanning tools for DNS records, and culminating in discovering a vast and unprecedented subdomain hijacking operation.</p><p id="2672" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mh mi gu mj b mk ml mm mn mo mp mq mr ms mt mu mv mw mx my mz na nb nc nd ne gn bk">The uncovered operation involves the manipulation of thousands of hijacked sub-domains belonging to or affiliated with big brands. Complex DNS manipulations for these domains allowed the dispatch of vast quantities of spammy and just outright malicious emails, falsely authorized under the guise of internationally recognized brands:</p><figure class="oq or os ot ou ov on oo paragraph-image"><div role="button" tabindex="0" class="ow ox fj oy bh oz"><div class="on oo op"><picture><source SubdoMaiiling</figcaption></figure><h1 id="445e" class="nk nl gu bf nm nn no np nq nr ns nt nu nv nw nx ny nz oa ob oc od oe of og oh bk">How a Clearly Scammy Email Passed Spam Filters</h1><p id="79b7" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mh mi gu mj b mk oi mm mn mo oj mq mr ms ok mu mv mw ol my mz na om nc nd ne gn bk">To start, let’s examine a telling example that encapsulates the entire scheme. We examine a particularly insidious email alerting of suspicious activity within a cloud storage account:</p><figure class="oq or os ot ou ov on oo paragraph-image"><div role="button" tabindex="0" class="ow ox fj oy bh oz"><div class="on oo pg"><picture><source srcSet="https://miro.medium.com/v2/resize:fit:640/format:webp/1*3YNT7e8H5-0hZ_abrjgQPA.png 640w, srcSet="https://miro.medium.com/v2/resize:fit:640/1*3YNT7e8H5-0hZ_abrjgQPA.png 640w, https://miro.medium.com/v2/resize:fit:720/1*3YNT7e8H5-0hZ_abrjgQPA.png 720w, https://miro.medium.com/v2/resize:fit:750/1*3YNT7e8H5-0hZ_abrjgQPA.png 750w, https://miro.medium.com/v2/resize:fit:786/1*3YNT7e8H5-0hZ_abrjgQPA.png 786w, https://miro.medium.com/v2/resize:fit:828/1*3YNT7e8H5-0hZ_abrjgQPA.png 828w, https://miro.medium.com/v2/resize:fit:1100/1*3YNT7e8H5-0hZ_abrjgQPA.png 1100w, (-webkit-min-device-pixel-ratio: 2.5) and (max-width: 700px) 80vw, (min-resolution: 2dppx) and (max-width: 700px) 100vw, (-webkit-min-device-pixel-ratio: 2) and (max-width: 700px) 100vw, 700px"/><img alt="" class="bh lo pa c" width="700" height="588" loading="lazy" role="presentation"/></picture></div></div><figcaption class="pb ff pc on oo pd pe bf b bg z du">Sample of a deceptive email — creating a sense of urgency over fake cloud storage warning</figcaption></figure><p id="343e" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mh mi gu mj b mk ml mm mn mo mp mq mr ms mt mu mv mw mx my mz na nb nc nd ne gn bk">Interacting with any part of this email, cleverly crafted as an image to dodge text-based spam filters, triggers a series of click-redirects through different domains. These redirects check your device type and geographic location, leading to content tailored to maximize profit. This could be anything from an annoying ad or affiliate link to more deceptive tactics like quiz scams, phishing sites, or even a malware download aimed at swindling you out of your money more directly.</p><p id="32f7" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mh mi gu mj b mk ml mm mn mo mp mq mr ms mt mu mv mw mx my mz na nb nc nd ne gn bk">We’ve encountered similar schemes before, but there was something distinct about this one. Initially, the question arose: how did an email like this pass authentication and security checks with major email providers and land right into the “Primary” inbox of users?</p><p id="1f90" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mh mi gu mj b mk ml mm mn mo mp mq mr ms mt mu mv mw mx my mz na nb nc nd ne gn bk">A closer look at this sample’s headers, especially the <code class="cx ph pi pj pk b">Authentication-Results</code> header revealed some intriguing insights:</p><pre class="oq or os ot ou pl pk pm bp pn bb bk"><span id="db21" class="po nl gu pk b bg pp pq l pr ps"><strong class="pk gv">dkim=pass</strong> @0091539714.2516999.2516999.healthylifes.uk.com&quot; header.i=@0091539714.2516999.2516999.healthylifes.uk.com header.s=selector1 header.b=YOsA3tIB;<br/><strong class="pk gv">spf=pass</strong> (google.com: domain of return_ulkvw@<strong class="pk gv">marthastewart.msn.com</strong> designates 62.244.33.18 as permitted sender);<br/><strong class="pk gv">dmarc=pass</strong> (p=QUARANTINE sp=NONE dis=NONE) header.from=uk.com</span></pre><p id="54cf" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mh mi gu mj b mk ml mm mn mo mp mq mr ms mt mu mv mw mx my mz na nb nc nd ne gn bk">Note the<strong class="mj gv"> SPF (Sender Policy Framework) </strong>Check<strong class="mj gv"> —</strong> SPF prevents email spoofing by verifying the email-sending server’s IP addresses against the domain’s authorized senders list. This one checks OK, as well as other industry standards:</p><ul class=""><li id="bbd5" class="mh mi gu mj b mk ml mm mn mo mp mq mr ms mt mu mv mw mx my mz na nb nc nd ne pt pu pv bk"><strong class="mj gv">DKIM</strong> — Successfully signing the authenticity of this email content with a cryptographic key provided by the sender at <code class="cx ph pi pj pk b">healthylifes.uk.com</code></li><li id="a69b" class="mh mi gu mj b mk pw mm mn mo px mq mr ms py mu mv mw pz my mz na qa nc nd ne pt pu pv bk"><strong class="mj gv">SMTP Server</strong> — that sent the email (<code class="cx ph pi pj pk b">62.244.33.18</code>) is located in <strong class="mj gv">Kyiv</strong>.</li><li id="a904" class="mh mi gu mj b mk pw mm mn mo px mq mr ms py mu mv mw pz my mz na qa nc nd ne pt pu pv bk"><strong class="mj gv">SPF</strong> — passes, with the domain <code class="cx ph pi pj pk b">marthastewart.msn.com</code> designating the SMTP Server IP address as legit.</li><li id="d3f7" class="mh mi gu mj b mk pw mm mn mo px mq mr ms py mu mv mw pz my mz na qa nc nd ne pt pu pv bk"><strong class="mj gv">DMARC</strong> — A domain-driven policy to enforce SPF and DKIM passes as well — following a policy of the top-level domain <code class="cx ph pi pj pk b">uk.com</code> stating “sp=NONE” (meaning no policy for subdomains)</li></ul><p id="c003" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mh mi gu mj b mk ml mm mn mo mp mq mr ms mt mu mv mw mx my mz na nb nc nd ne gn bk"><strong class="mj gv">Hold on!</strong> What do Martha Stewart and Microsoft’s MSN have to do with authorizing this scammy email?!</p><figure class="oq or os ot ou ov on oo paragraph-image"><div role="button" tabindex="0" class="ow ox fj oy bh oz"><div class="on oo qb"><picture><source srcSet="https://miro.medium.com/v2/resize:fit:640/format:webp/1*e6ZsLIos22vUHCXiztDP1Q.png 640w, https://miro.medium.com/v2/resize:fit:720/format:webp/1*e6ZsLIos22vUHCXiztDP1Q.png alt="" class="bh lo pa c" width="700" height="530" loading="lazy" role="presentation"/></picture></div></div></figure><h1 id="6fb9" class="nk nl gu bf nm nn no np nq nr ns nt nu nv nw nx ny nz oa ob oc od oe of og oh bk">Resurrecting 2001 Martha Stewart’s Sweepstakes</h1><p id="0265" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mh mi gu mj b mk oi mm mn mo oj mq mr ms ok mu mv mw ol my mz na om nc nd ne gn bk">Here comes the interesting bit. The deceptive Cloud storage email originated from an SMTP server in Kyiv, yet it was flagged as being sent from <code class="cx ph pi pj pk b">Return_UlKvw@marthastewart.msn.com</code>. On the surface, this might seem legitimate — similar to how businesses use mass mailing services to send emails on their behalf, which requires authorizing those services to use their names.</p><p id="d4a3" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mh mi gu mj b mk ml mm mn mo mp mq mr ms mt mu mv mw mx my mz na nb nc nd ne gn bk">However, in this scenario, a subdomain of <code class="cx ph pi pj pk b">msn.com</code> authorized the SMTP server at <code class="cx ph pi pj pk b">62.244.33.18</code> to send emails, raising questions about the legitimacy of this approval process.</p><p id="019a" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mh mi gu mj b mk ml mm mn mo mp mq mr ms mt mu mv mw mx my mz na nb nc nd ne gn bk">Could someone at MSN incorrectly type a wrong IP address in the SPF record, or perhaps a deliberate act of a malicious insider? A closer examination of the <a class="af nf" href="https://www.whatsmydns.net/dns-lookup?query=marthastewart.msn.com&amp;server=google" rel="noopener ugc nofollow" target="_blank">DNS record</a> for <code class="cx ph pi pj pk b">marthastewart.msn.com</code> offers some revealing insights.</p><pre class="oq or os ot ou pl pk pm bp pn bb bk"><span id="83ad" class="po nl gu pk b bg pp pq l pr ps"><strong class="pk gv">marthastewart.msn.com</strong>. 3600 IN CNAME <strong class="pk gv">msnmarthastewartsweeps.com</strong>.</span></pre><p id="08f8" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mh mi gu mj b mk ml mm mn mo mp mq mr ms mt mu mv mw mx my mz na nb nc nd ne gn bk">This subdomain is linked to another domain with that CNAME record. This means that the subdomain inherits the entire behavior of <code class="cx ph pi pj pk b">msnmarthastewartsweeps.com</code> , including it’s SPF policy.</p><p id="829a" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mh mi gu mj b mk ml mm mn mo mp mq mr ms mt mu mv mw mx my mz na nb nc nd ne gn bk">And so, examining <code class="cx ph pi pj pk b">msnmarthastewartsweeps.com</code> will show us this SPF policy under one of its TXT records:</p><pre class="oq or os ot ou pl pk pm bp pn bb bk"><span id="7a1a" class="po nl gu pk b bg pp pq l pr ps">&quot;v=spf1 include:<strong class="pk gv">harrisburgjetcenter.com</strong> include:<strong class="pk gv">greaterversatile.com</strong> -all&quot;</span></pre><p id="6de6" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mh mi gu mj b mk ml mm mn mo mp mq mr ms mt mu mv mw mx my mz na nb nc nd ne gn bk">The SPF record above is quite interesting, as it uses the <code class="cx ph pi pj pk b">include:</code> syntax that allows expanding the IP list of approved senders using other domains’ SPF records — up to 10 recursive domain resolves that are allowed by the protocol. So happen to be, there are tons of IP addresses under those domains and exactly 10 more included domains. So, if we recursively query this SPF record, we end up with a massive list of <strong class="mj gv">17826 IPs</strong>! And yes, <code class="cx ph pi pj pk b">62.244.33.18</code> is indeed included:</p><figure class="oq or os ot ou ov on oo paragraph-image"><div role="button" tabindex="0" class="ow ox fj oy bh genuine subdomain to a hijacked, spam-authenticating, MSN sub-domain</code></figcaption></figure><p id="603f" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mh mi gu mj b mk ml mm mn mo mp mq mr ms mt mu mv mw mx my mz na nb nc nd ne gn bk">Now, the domain is owned by a specific actor that has control over its DNS records and, as a consequence, controls the MSN subdomain record as well! So, in this case, the actor can send emails to anyone they wish as if <code class="cx ph pi pj pk b">msn.com</code> and their approved mailers sent those emails!</p><h1 id="4a1d" class="nk nl gu bf nm nn no np nq nr ns nt nu nv nw nx ny nz oa ob oc od oe of og oh bk">Classic Subdomain Hijacking Danger</h1><p id="b518" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mh mi gu mj b mk oi mm mn mo oj mq mr ms ok mu mv mw ol my mz na om nc nd ne gn bk">This is a CLASSIC subdomain hijacking scheme. A campaigner constantly scans and enumerates domains for long-forgotten subdomains with dangling CNAME records of abandoned domains. Quickly register these domains again — and you have control!</p><p id="c51d" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mh mi gu mj b mk ml mm mn mo mp mq mr ms mt mu mv mw mx my mz na nb nc nd ne gn bk">Think about the dangers this simple hijack activity can call for. Here, SPF records were abused to send malicious emails. But why stop there? One can also create an SMTP server under this hijacked hostname and send the emails directly from this domain.</p><p id="7179" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mh mi gu mj b mk ml mm mn mo mp mq mr ms mt mu mv mw mx my mz na nb nc nd ne gn bk">And why stop with emails? A reputable subdomain is such a valuable asset for hackers— think about a classic Microsoft login phishing page served under the MSN domain… “<strong class="mj gv">Priceless</strong>”….</p><figure class="oq or os ot ou ov on oo paragraph-image"><div role="button" tabindex="0" class="ow ox fj oy bh oz"><div class="on oo qc"><picture><source srcSet="https://miro.medium.com/v2/resize:fit:640/format:webp/1*UK44e5EdxTWkkp9LEsp12g.png 640w, https://miro.medium.com/v2/resize:fit:720/format:webp/1*UK44e5EdxTWkkp9LEsp12g.png 720w, https://miro.medium.com/v2/resize:fit:1400/1*UK44e5EdxTWkkp9LEsp12g.png 1400w" sizes="(min-resolution: 4dppx) and (max-width: 700px) 50vw, (-webkit-min-device-pixel-ratio: 4) and (max-width: 700px) 50vw, (min-resolution: 3dppx) and (max-width: 700px) 67vw, (-webkit-min-device-pixel-ratio: 3) and (max-width: 700px) 65vw, (min-resolution: 2.5dppx) and (max-width: 700px) 80vw, (-webkit-min-device-pixel-ratio: 2.5) and (max-width: 700px) 80vw, (min-resolution: 2dppx) and (max-width: 700px) 100vw, (-webkit-min-device-pixel-ratio: 2) and (max-width: 700px) 100vw, 700px"/><img alt="" class="bh lo pa c" width="700" height="358" loading="lazy" role="presentation"/></picture></div></div><figcaption class="pb ff pc on oo pd pe bf b bg z du"><strong class="bf nm">Concept Only</strong> — Demonstrate how quickly a hijacked domain can turn even more evil</figcaption></figure><p id="b362" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mh mi gu mj b mk ml mm mn mo mp mq mr ms mt mu mv mw mx my mz na nb nc nd ne gn bk">Though we have no records of any of the “CNAME-hijacked” sub-domains being abused for malicious phishing page hosting… yet… this is possible with just a click of a button and raises great concerns!</p><h1 id="7f8b" class="nk nl gu bf nm nn no np nq nr ns nt nu nv nw nx ny nz oa ob oc od oe of og oh bk">SPF-Takeover — Another Tactic Uncovered</h1><p id="fbf3" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mh mi gu mj b mk oi mm mn mo oj mq mr ms ok mu mv mw ol my mz na om nc nd ne gn bk">When we continued exploring more cases like this, we became quite horrified, to say the least. We found thousands of active cases like this with clear indications that this operation has been ongoing for at least<strong class="mj gv"> two years!</strong></p><p id="7811" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mh mi gu mj b mk ml mm mn mo mp mq mr ms mt mu mv mw mx my mz na nb nc nd ne gn bk"><strong class="mj gv">CNAME-takeover</strong> is not the only type of abuse we’ve found sending those emails. Another interesting one is what we call <strong class="mj gv">SPF-Takeover</strong>.</p><p id="66a5" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mh mi gu mj b mk ml mm mn mo mp mq mr ms mt mu mv mw mx my mz na nb nc nd ne gn bk"><strong class="mj gv">SPF-Takeover — </strong>Similar to dangling CNAME records, we also see numerous cases in which the SPF record of a known domain holds abandoned domains of old email/marketing-related services that were probably dismissed or just gone out of business. Quickly grabbing ownership of those domains will allow the attacker to inject their IPs into that domain’s SPF records easily, and this time using the <strong class="mj gv">main domain name</strong> as the sender!</p><p id="d1ef" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mh mi gu mj b mk ml mm mn mo mp mq mr ms mt mu mv mw mx my mz na nb nc nd ne gn bk">For example, let’s quickly analyze the DNS records of the well-known watch brand <code class="cx ph pi pj pk b">www.swatch.com</code> that was also found to be compromised:</p><figure class="oq or os ot ou ov on oo paragraph-image"><div role="button" tabindex="0" class="ow ox fj oy bh oz"><div class="on oo qe"><picture><source srcSet="https://miro.medium.com/v2/resize:fit:640/format:webp/1*AUKK6RabbaYWVW7_w8RoOA.png 640w, dangling, now hijacked, domain in its SPF record</figcaption></figure><p id="d382" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mh mi gu mj b mk ml mm mn mo mp mq mr ms mt mu mv mw mx my mz na nb nc nd ne gn bk">As of today, the TXT Record of this domain name holds this SPF configuration:</p><pre class="oq or os ot ou pl pk pm bp pn bb bk"><span id="877a" class="po nl gu pk b bg pp pq l pr ps">&quot;v=spf1 mx a a:<strong class="pk gv">directtoaccess.com</strong> ip4:80.120.59.245 ip4:82.98.75.216 include:spf.swisscom.com include:spf.mail.netrics.ch ip4:195.78.51.100 ip4:109.74.206.22 ip4:46.254.32.37 ip6:2a01:7e00::f03c:91ff:fe84:6b60 a:production.eu01.swatch.demandware.net a:staging.eu01.swatch.demandware.net include:spf.recruitmail.com -all&quot;</span></pre><p id="3e65" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mh mi gu mj b mk ml mm mn mo mp mq mr ms mt mu mv mw mx my mz na nb nc nd ne gn bk">This is a long and complicated SPF record with both hardcoded IPs as well as other domains — all approved to send emails on behalf of <code class="cx ph pi pj pk b">swatch.com</code>. We can see those included domains are originating from marketing and data services, and that’s for a reason — those will send emails in the name of <code class="cx ph pi pj pk b">swatch.com</code> as part of their genuine functionality.</p><p id="1669" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mh mi gu mj b mk ml mm mn mo mp mq mr ms mt mu mv mw mx my mz na nb nc nd ne gn bk">You need just one abandoned domain to hit the jackpot, and this one is <code class="cx ph pi pj pk b">directtoaccess.com</code>. Note it is included under the prefix <code class="cx ph pi pj pk b">a:</code> meaning all “A” DNS records of that domain (IP addresses of hosting servers) are allowed to send emails. So it won’t be a shock to see that this specific domain now holds <a class="af nf" href="https://www.whatsmydns.net/dns-lookup?query=directtoaccess.com&amp;server=google" rel="noopener ugc nofollow" target="_blank">way too many IP addresses</a> in its A record — 81, to be exact. This is, of course, quite an abnormal behavior:</p><pre class="oq or os ot ou pl pk pm bp pn bb bk"><span id="06e4" class="po nl gu pk b bg pp pq l pr ps">;QUESTION<br/>directtoaccess.com. IN A<br/>;ANSWER<br/>directtoaccess.com. 3600 IN A 51.81.215.32<br/>directtoaccess.com. 3600 IN A 51.81.215.33<br/>directtoaccess.com. 3600 IN A 51.81.215.34<br/>..<br/>....<br/>[76 More]<br/>......<br/>.........<br/>directtoaccess.com. 3600 IN A 104.223.43.170<br/>directtoaccess.com. 3600 IN A 104.223.43.171<br/>;AUTHORITY<br/>;ADDITIONAL</span></pre><p id="a310" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mh mi gu mj b mk ml mm mn mo mp mq mr ms mt mu mv mw mx my mz na nb nc nd ne gn bk">What is (or was…) <code class="cx ph pi pj pk b">directtoaccess.com</code> anyways? Going back in time to 2006 reveals the true story:</p><figure class="oq or os ot ou ov on oo paragraph-image"><div role="button" tabindex="0" class="ow ox fj oy bh oz"><div class="on oo b">directtoaccess.com — as captured by the Internet Archive back in 2006</code></figcaption></figure><p id="b5e6" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mh mi gu mj b mk ml mm mn mo mp mq mr ms mt mu mv mw mx my mz na nb nc nd ne gn bk">This domain is a direct credit card service of some kind, long forgotten since 2006, when it was last active. But today, this domain is once again registered by Namecheap. Yes, <strong class="mj gv">Namecheap</strong> again.</p><h1 id="c34f" class="nk nl gu bf nm nn no np nq nr ns nt nu nv nw nx ny nz oa ob oc od oe of og oh bk">From Subdomain Hijacking to Mass “SubdoMailing”</h1><p id="90af" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mh mi gu mj b mk oi mm mn mo oj mq mr ms ok mu mv mw ol my mz na om nc nd ne gn bk">Given these discoveries, it became evident that we were observing a highly coordinated campaign rather than random acts of domain hijacking. This operation is meticulously designed to misuse these assets for distributing various malevolent “Advertisements,” aiming to generate as many clicks as possible for this “Ad network” clients.</p><p id="fe77" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mh mi gu mj b mk ml mm mn mo mp mq mr ms mt mu mv mw mx my mz na nb nc nd ne gn bk">This is not another mass mailing campaign; this is “<strong class="mj gv">SubdoMailing</strong>”!</p><p id="750a" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mh mi gu mj b mk ml mm mn mo mp mq mr ms mt mu mv mw mx my mz na nb nc nd ne gn bk">Notably, the exploitation of CNAME and SPF-Based hijacking extends beyond mere SPF authentication. Once overtaken, these assets are leveraged in multiple facets, all converging towards the central objective of this campaign: to maximize email-oriented ad clicks.</p><ul class=""><li id="73af" class="mh mi gu mj b mk ml mm mn mo mp mq mr ms mt mu mv mw mx my mz na nb nc nd ne pt pu pv bk"><strong class="mj gv">SPF Authentication</strong> — Injecting SPF-approved IP addresses of actor-owned SMTP servers.</li><li id="595d" class="mh mi gu mj b mk pw mm mn mo px mq mr ms py mu mv mw pz my mz na qa nc nd ne pt pu pv bk"><strong class="mj gv">SMTP Servers</strong> — Hosting SMTP servers under the hijacked subdomain to send mass emails.</li><li id="5e92" class="mh mi gu mj b mk pw mm mn mo px mq mr ms py mu mv mw pz my mz na qa nc nd ne pt pu pv bk"><strong class="mj gv">Hosting Click-Redirection</strong> — Hosting redirectors and click-analysis links for the actual ads, including images and other assets for email content.</li><li id="2456" class="mh mi gu mj b mk pw mm mn mo px mq mr ms py mu mv mw pz my mz na qa nc nd ne pt pu pv bk"><strong class="mj gv">“Unsubscribe” Pages</strong> — Due to regulations, those assets also host generic (probably haywire) unsubscribe pages to try and seem as legit as possible.</li><li id="5458" class="mh mi gu mj b mk pw mm mn mo px mq mr ms py mu mv mw pz my mz na qa nc nd ne pt pu pv bk"><strong class="mj gv">From Address</strong> — in some cases, those emails are leveraged to be set as being sent from those hijacked domains! In many cases, they also abuse poor DMARC policies set on those domains as well.</li></ul><h1 id="24df" class="nk nl gu bf nm nn no np nq nr ns nt nu nv nw nx ny nz oa ob oc od oe of og oh bk">“ResurrecAds” Threat Actor — Uncovered</h1><p id="4e77" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mh mi gu mj b mk oi mm mn mo oj mq mr ms ok mu mv mw ol my mz na om nc nd ne gn bk">Leveraging our comprehensive data, detection methods, and ongoing DNS and Whois scans, we’ve identified thousands of instances of “Subdomailing”, encompassing both CNAME and SPF-based tactics, from the last 60 days of activity. This extensive analysis revealed many spammy and malicious emails, ranging from counterfeit package delivery alerts to outright phishing for account credentials, some of which were even dispatched directly from the hijacked subdomains.</p><figure class="oq or os ot ou ov on oo paragraph-image"><div role="button" tabindex="0" class="ow ox fj oy bh oz"><div class="on oo qg"><picture><source alt="" class="bh lo pa c" width="700" height="414" loading="lazy" role="presentation"/></picture></div></div><figcaption class="pb ff pc on oo pd pe bf b bg z du">VMware alerts on compromised iCloud account? Cellcom (ISP) alerts on failed Facebook login? Right…</figcaption></figure><p id="e04e" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mh mi gu mj b mk ml mm mn mo mp mq mr ms mt mu mv mw mx my mz na nb nc nd ne gn bk">The evidence we’ve gathered points to the likelihood of a single main threat actor behind this extensive operation. This entity appears to be systematically scanning the internet for vulnerable domains, identifying opportunities, purchasing domains, securing hosts and IP addresses and then meticulously orchestrating the ongoing campaign of email dissemination. This involves a vast network of both hijacked and deliberately acquired domain and IP assets, indicating a high level of organization and technical sophistication in maintaining this broad scale of operations.</p><p id="c8df" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mh mi gu mj b mk ml mm mn mo mp mq mr ms mt mu mv mw mx my mz na nb nc nd ne gn bk">Here at <a class="af nf" href="https://www.guard.io" rel="noopener ugc nofollow" target="_blank">Guardio</a>, we’ve been closely monitoring a threat actor we call “<strong class="mj gv">ResurrecAds</strong>”, highlighting their covert motive to profit as an Ad-Network entity while employing the dark tactics described in this paper. Central to their operation is the strategy of reviving “dead” domains of or affiliated with big brands, using them as backdoors to exploit legitimate services and brands. This approach enables them to circumvent contemporary email protection measures, showcasing their adeptness at manipulating the digital advertising ecosystem for nefarious gains.</p><figure class="oq or os ot ou ov on oo paragraph-image"><div role="button" tabindex="0" class="ow ox fj oy bh oz"><div class="on oo qc"><picture><source srcSet="https://miro.medium.com/v2/resize:fit:640/format:webp/1*9RJyV26VRjRngPUD97RrGQ.png 640w, https://miro.medium.com/v2/resize:fit:720/format:webp/1*9RJyV26VRjRngPUD97RrGQ.png 720w, works</figcaption></figure><p id="156c" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mh mi gu mj b mk ml mm mn mo mp mq mr ms mt mu mv mw mx my mz na nb nc nd ne gn bk">Armed with a vast collection of compromised reputable domains, servers, and IP addresses, this Ad-Network deftly navigates through the malicious email propagation process, seamlessly switching and hopping among its assets at will.</p><h1 id="c766" class="nk nl gu bf nm nn no np nq nr ns nt nu nv nw nx ny nz oa ob oc od oe of og oh bk">Tracking IOCs and Connecting Loose Ends</h1><p id="d3ec" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mh mi gu mj b mk oi mm mn mo oj mq mr ms ok mu mv mw ol my mz na om nc nd ne gn bk">Some of the most common indications of compromise (IOCs) that tie different “SubdoMailing” cases to one another include visual references and the re-use of assets and concepts.</p><p id="7a81" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mh mi gu mj b mk ml mm mn mo mp mq mr ms mt mu mv mw mx my mz na nb nc nd ne gn bk">The most common are the templates used for generic landing pages and fake unsubscribe pages, which are usually hosted on the SMTP servers sending those emails. The text is always the same; only the design changes from time to time:</p><figure class="oq or os ot ou ov on oo paragraph-image"><div role="button" tabindex="0" class="ow ox fj oy bh oz"><div bk">In most cases, those servers also share the same network fingerprint —with SMTP (25) and HTTP (80) ports open, yet also include a very specific port 3128 running the “Squid” HTTP Proxy. The last is probably used for remote management and quickly “mirroring” between different SMTP servers for central control. by this threat actor to send emails</figcaption></figure><h1 id="9ea9" class="nk nl gu bf nm nn no np nq nr ns nt nu nv nw nx ny nz oa ob oc od oe of og oh bk">SubdoMailing — In Numbers</h1><p id="dfdc" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mh mi gu mj b mk oi mm mn mo oj mq mr ms ok mu mv mw ol my mz na om nc nd ne gn bk">Upon unraveling this malicious scheme, the sheer scale of the operation became apparent. It extends far beyond the thousands of compromised domains and DNS records previously identified. “<strong class="mj gv">ResurrecAds</strong>” manages an extensive infrastructure encompassing a wide array of hosts, SMTP servers, IP addresses, and even private residential ISP connections, alongside many additional owned domain names.</p><blockquote class="ng nh ni"><p id="220c" class="mh mi nj mj b mk ml mm mn mo mp mq mr ms mt mu mv mw mx my mz na nb nc nd ne gn bk"><strong class="mj gv">We see a sophisticated distribution architecture that supports this vast network of servers and domain assets, designed carefully to disseminate millions of malicious emails daily, aimed at spam proliferation and of course, click monetization.</strong></p></blockquote><p id="610b" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mh mi gu mj b mk ml mm mn mo mp mq mr ms mt mu mv mw mx my mz na nb nc nd ne gn bk">Diving into the numbers and aggregated data provides us with a clearer view of both the immense scale and the modus operandi of this threat actor:</p><figure class="oq or os ot ou ov on oo paragraph-image"><div role="button" tabindex="0" class="ow ox fj oy bh oz"><div class="on oo days of activity</figcaption></figure><p id="8a02" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mh mi gu mj b mk ml mm mn mo mp mq mr ms mt mu mv mw mx my mz na nb nc nd ne gn bk">Looking at the registration dates of compromised domains proves without a doubt that this operation has been <strong class="mj gv">ongoing since late 2022, at least</strong>. These registration dates are of the CNAME-linked or SPF-included dangling domains that were abandoned and then re-registered by this threat actor. Almost all are registered with a single domain registration service — <strong class="mj gv">Namecheap</strong>, known for being the <a class="af nf" href="https://krebsonsecurity.com/2018/06/bad-men-at-work-please-dont-click" rel="noopener ugc nofollow" target="_blank">house of many of the most scammy TLDs</a>.</p><p id="c23e" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mh mi gu mj b mk ml mm mn mo mp mq mr ms mt mu mv mw mx my mz na nb nc nd ne gn bk">Note that the domain age is reset upon re-registration. volume per day — peaking at around June 2023</figcaption></figure><p id="f20d" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mh mi gu mj b mk ml mm mn mo mp mq mr ms mt mu mv mw mx my mz na nb nc nd ne gn bk">Delving into the exploitation of these compromised domains, we uncover the actor’s strategic “<strong class="mj gv">domain economy</strong>.” This approach maximizes resource utility while minimizing detection and depletion risks, allowing for their prolonged use. A prime domain in the control of this “Ad network” sees action briefly, typically for just 1–2 days, followed by significant intervals of inactivity. Meanwhile, the actor rotates through other assets. sub-domain activities in the past 60 days</figcaption></figure><p id="f5c0" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mh mi gu mj b mk ml mm mn mo mp mq mr ms mt mu mv mw mx my mz na nb nc nd ne gn bk">Similarly, SMTP servers are shuffled from one IP address to another, often resulting in a single hijacked sub-domain dispatching emails from multiple global locations within a single day. This tactic is crucial for the threat actor to avoid overexposing a specific IP address, ensuring the email distribution is spread out through various servers worldwide, thus maintaining the stealth and longevity of their operations:</p><figure class="oq or os ot ou ov on oo paragraph-image"><div role="button" tabindex="0" class="ow ox fj oy bh oz"><div class="on oo qj"><picture><source srcSet="https://miro.medium.com/v2/resize:fit:640/format:webp/1*shQYks83ENKhpnS0HY1EWA.png 640w, https://miro.medium.com/v2/resize:fit:720/format:webp/1*shQYks83ENKhpnS0HY1EWA.png 720w, alt="" class="bh lo pa c" width="700" height="349" loading="lazy" role="presentation"/></picture></div></div><figcaption class="pb ff pc on oo pd pe bf b bg z du">A hijacked taxact.com subdomain sending emails from 22 different IPs worldwide in one activity day</figcaption></figure><p id="1fe1" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mh mi gu mj b mk ml mm mn mo mp mq mr ms mt mu mv mw mx my mz na nb nc nd ne gn bk">Given these sophisticated tactics, we’re clearly facing a formidable operation characterized by significant expenditure and substantial revenue. Our investigation has yet to pinpoint the exact origin of this operation, but our efforts to uncover the source continue. What remains within our power and responsibility is to mount a defense and fight back — this time with your help!</p></div></div></div><div class="ab cb qk ql qm qn" role="separator"><span class="qo by bm qp qq qr"></span><span class="qo by bm qp qq qr"></span><span class="qo by bm qp qq"></span></div><div class="gn go gp gq gr"><div class="ab cb"><div class="ci bh fz ga gb gc"><h1 id="8a2c" class="nk nl gu bf nm nn qs np nq nr qt nt nu nv qu nx ny nz qv ob oc od qw of og oh bk">The State of the (Spammer’s) Union</h1><p id="84b0" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mh mi gu mj b mk oi mm mn mo oj mq mr ms ok mu mv mw ol my mz na om nc nd ne gn bk">Email may just be one of the earliest forms of digital communication applied on the internet, and though it’s been more than 50 years since the first email was sent, it is still one of the main applications to be used — <strong class="mj gv">and abused</strong>.</p><p id="343f" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mh mi gu mj b mk ml mm mn mo mp mq mr ms mt mu mv mw mx my mz na nb nc nd ne gn bk">The Fight against spammers and impersonators introduced several security and authentication methods, with SPF and DKIM back in 2004, following DMARC policy in 2012. All of those remained optional — until <a class="af nf" href="https://blog.google/products/gmail/gmail-security-authentication-spam-protection/" rel="noopener ugc nofollow" target="_blank">Google stepped up</a> last year, requiring at least ONE of the optional authentication methods to pass. This will also become mandatory for mass mailers (sending more than 5000 emails daily) later this year.</p><p id="fd33" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mh mi gu mj b mk ml mm mn mo mp mq mr ms mt mu mv mw mx my mz na nb nc nd ne gn bk"><strong class="mj gv">What does this mean for the dark ecosystem of spam and phishing?</strong> For average spammers, this might mean a slight adjustment in tactics — securing domain authenticity and adding an unsubscribe option. However, these measures are hardly a hurdle for emails sourced from low-reputation domains bought by spammers in masses, which are also easily blocked.</p><p id="ebc5" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mh mi gu mj b mk ml mm mn mo mp mq mr ms mt mu mv mw mx my mz na nb nc nd ne gn bk">Yet, the upcoming changes signal a significant shift for those engaged in more sinister activities — distributing unethical ads, spreading fake news, launching phishing attacks, and propagating malware.</p><blockquote class="ng nh ni"><p id="661c" class="mh mi nj mj b mk ml mm mn mo mp mq mr ms mt mu mv mw mx my mz na nb nc nd ne gn bk"><strong class="mj gv">Our research has revealed that threat actors are not merely reacting to security measures; they’ve been proactively adapting and evolving for some time. A significant part of their strategy involves exploiting the reputations and resources of legitimate domains to disseminate malicious content under the guise of trustworthy sources.</strong></p></blockquote><p id="081c" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mh mi gu mj b mk ml mm mn mo mp mq mr ms mt mu mv mw mx my mz na nb nc nd ne gn bk">Website owners find themselves unwitting participants in these schemes, underscoring their shared duty to combat these threats actively. It’s crucial to regularly check your domains for signs of compromise and better manage your online assets — starting with removing unused subdomains and DNS records. By securing our websites and raising awareness, we can make the digital landscape a more challenging environment for scammers, tipping the scales in our favor.</p></div></div></div><div class="ab cb qk ql qm qn" role="separator"><span class="qo by bm qp qq qr"></span><span class="qo by bm qp qq qr"></span><span class="qo by bm qp qq"></span></div><div class="gn go gp gq gr"><div class="ab cb"><div class="ci bh fz ga gb gc"><h1 id="7d67" class="nk nl gu bf nm nn qs np nq nr qt nt nu nv qu nx ny nz qv ob oc od qw of og oh bk">Fighting Back!</h1><p id="9e58" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mh mi gu mj b mk oi mm mn mo oj mq mr ms ok mu mv mw ol my mz na om nc nd ne gn bk">At Guardio, our mission extends beyond merely detecting and blocking malicious emails for our customers. We are also committed to eradicating this issue at its source, aiming to dismantle the infrastructure that fuels such nefarious activities.</p><p id="dc5e" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mh mi gu mj b mk ml mm mn mo mp mq mr ms mt mu mv mw mx my mz na nb nc nd ne gn bk">We decided to create a special <a class="af nf" href="http://www.guard.io/subdomailing" rel="noopener ugc nofollow" target="_blank">“SubdoMailing” checker website</a>, allowing domain administrators and site owners to quickly check if any trace of abuse was found by our systems — and get the relevant info needed to fix and prevent this.</p><figure class="oq or os ot ou ov on oo paragraph-image"><div role="button" tabindex="0" class="ow ox fj oy bh oz"><div class="on oo op"><picture><source
